<p><b>CoRoT</b> (French: <span lang="fr"><b>CO</b>nvection <b>RO</b>tation et <b>T</b>ransits planétaires</span>; English: COnvection ROtation and planetary Transits) is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_observatory" title="Space observatory">space observatory</a> mission led by the <a href="/wiki/CNES" title="CNES">French Space Agency (CNES)</a> in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency (ESA)</a> and other international partners. The mission's two objectives are to search for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a> with short orbital periods, particularly those of large <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial size</a>, and to perform <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a> by measuring <a href="/wiki/Solar-like_oscillations" title="Solar-like oscillations">solar-like oscillations</a> in stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAPR2006_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESAPR2006-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was launched at 14:28:00 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> on 27 December 2006, atop a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soyuz_2_rocket" title="Soyuz 2 rocket">Soyuz 2.1b</a> rocket,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNESMay2007_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNESMay2007-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2006_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2006-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> reporting <a href="/wiki/First_light_(astronomy)" title="First light (astronomy)">first light</a> on 18 January 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hellemans2007_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hellemans2007-6">[6]</a></sup> Subsequently, the probe started to collect science data on 2 February 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> CoRoT is the first <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> dedicated to the detection of <a href="/wiki/Methods_of_detecting_exoplanets" title="Methods of detecting exoplanets">transiting extrasolar planets</a>, opening the way for more advanced probes such as <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler</a> as well as future missions such as <a href="/wiki/Transiting_Exoplanet_Survey_Satellite" title="Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite">TESS</a> and <a href="/wiki/PLATO_(spacecraft)" title="PLATO (spacecraft)">PLATO</a>. It detected its first extrasolar planet, <a href="/wiki/COROT-1b" title="COROT-1b">COROT-1b</a>, in May 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAFirstPlanet_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESAFirstPlanet-8">[8]</a></sup> just 3 months after the start of the observations. Mission flight operations were originally scheduled to end 2.5 years from launch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COROTSat_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-COROTSat-9">[9]</a></sup> but operations were extended to 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corot2013_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corot2013-10">[10]</a></sup> On 2 November 2012, CoRoT suffered a computer failure that made it impossible to retrieve any data from its telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> After repair attempts, on 24 June 2013, it was announced that CoRoT has been retired and would be decommissioned; lowered in orbit to allow it to burn up in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceDaily-2013-06-24_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceDaily-2013-06-24-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_design">Spacecraft design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Spacecraft design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The CoRoT optical design minimizes stray light coming from the Earth and provides a <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a> of 2.7° by 3.05°. The CoRoT optical path consists of a 27 cm (10.6 in) diameter off-axis <a href="/wiki/Afocal_system" title="Afocal system">afocal</a> telescope housed in a two-stage <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opaque baffle</a> specifically designed to block sunlight reflected by the Earth and a camera consisting of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioptric" title="Dioptric">dioptric</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Objective_lens" title="Objective lens">objective</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Focal_plane" title="Focal plane">focal box</a>. Inside the focal box is an array of four <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> detectors protected against <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">radiation</a> by aluminum <a href="/wiki/Radiation_protection" title="Radiation protection">shielding</a> 10mm thick. The <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a> CCDs are defocused by 760μm toward the dioptric objective to avoid saturation of the brightest stars. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prism_(optics)" title="Prism (optics)">prism</a> in front of the planet detection CCDs gives a small <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectrum</a> designed to disperse more strongly in the blue wavelengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-design_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-design-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corot_Focal_plane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Corot_Focal_plane.jpg/220px-Corot_Focal_plane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Corot_Focal_plane.jpg/330px-Corot_Focal_plane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Corot_Focal_plane.jpg/440px-Corot_Focal_plane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corot_Focal_plane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The focal plane of CoRoT with its four full frame transfer CCD. The dark zone corresponds to the photo-sensitive area. Two CCDs are dedicated to the exoplanet programme and the two other ones to the asteroseismology programme.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> detectors are model 4280 CCDs provided by <a class="external text" href="http://www.e2v.com/" rel="nofollow">E2V Technologies</a>. These CCDs are frame-transfer, thinned, back-illuminated designs in a 2048 pixel by 2048 pixel array. Each pixel is 13.5 × 13.5μm<sup>2</sup> in size which corresponds to an angular pixel size of 2.32 arcsec. The CCDs are cooled to −40 °C (233.2 K; −40.0 °F). These detectors are arranged in a square pattern with two each dedicated to the planetary detection and <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a>. The data output stream from the CCDs are connected in two <i>chains</i>. Each chain has one planetary detection CCD and one <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a> CCD. The <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a> for planetary detection is 3.5°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-design_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-design-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COROT_integration.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1769" data-file-width="2653" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/COROT_integration.jpg/220px-COROT_integration.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/COROT_integration.jpg/330px-COROT_integration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/COROT_integration.jpg/440px-COROT_integration.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COROT_integration.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The CoRoT satellite in the integration hall of Thales Alenia Space, Cannes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The satellite, built in the <a href="/wiki/Cannes_Mandelieu_Space_Center" title="Cannes Mandelieu Space Center">Cannes Mandelieu Space Center</a>, has a launch mass of 630 kg, is 4.10 m long, 1.984 m in diameter and is powered by two solar panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-COROTSat_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-COROTSat-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_design">Mission design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mission design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_current_red.svg"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(May 2016)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The satellite observes perpendicular to its orbital plane, meaning there will be no Earth <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultations</a>, allowing up to 150 days of continuous observation. These observation sessions, called "Long Runs", allow detection of smaller and long-period planets. During the remaining 30 days between the two main observation periods, CoRoT observes other patches of sky for a few weeks long "Short Runs", in order to analyze a larger number of stars for the asteroseismic program. After the loss of half the field of view due to <a href="/wiki/Failure" title="Failure">failure</a> of Data Processing Unit No. 1 in March 2009, the observation strategy changed to 3 months observing runs, in order to optimize the number of observed stars and detection efficiency.</p>
<p>In order to avoid the Sun entering in its field of view, during the northern summer CoRoT will observe in an area around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpens_Cauda" title="Serpens Cauda">Serpens Cauda</a>, toward the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_center" title="Galactic center">galactic center</a>, and during the winter it will observe in <a href="/wiki/Monoceros" title="Monoceros">Monoceros</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_anticenter" title="Galactic anticenter">Galactic anticenter</a>. Both these "eyes" of CoRoT have been studied in preliminary observations carried out between 1998 and 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> allowing the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">database</a>, called COROTSKY,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> with data about the stars located in these two patches of sky. This allows selecting the best fields for observation: the exoplanet research program requires a large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_stars" title="Dwarf stars">dwarf stars</a> to be monitored, and to avoid <a href="/wiki/Giant_star" title="Giant star">giant stars</a>, for which planetary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">transits</a> are too shallow to be detectable. The asteroseismic program requires stars brighter than magnitude 9, and to cover as many different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Star_classification" title="Star classification">types of stars</a> as possible. In addition, in order to optimize the observations, the fields shouldn't be too sparse – fewer targets observed – or too crowded – too many stars overlapping. Several fields have been already observed:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>IRa01</b>, from 18 January 2007 to 3 April 2007 – 9879 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>SRc01</b>, from 3 April 2007 to 9 May 2007 – 6975 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRc01</b>, from 9 May 2007 to 15 October 2007 – 11408 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRa01</b>, from 15 October 2007 to 3 March 2008 – 11408 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>SRa01</b>, from 3 March 2008 to 31 March 2008 – 8150 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRc02</b>, from 31 March 2008 to 8 September 2008 – 11408 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>SRc02</b>, from 8 September 2008 to 6 October 2008 – 11408 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>SRa02</b>, from 6 October 2008 to 12 November 2008 – 10265 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRa02</b>, from 12 November 2008 to 30 March 2009 – 11408 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRc03</b>, from 30 March 2009 to 2 July 2009 – 5661 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRc04</b>, from 2 July 2009 to 30 September 2009 – 5716 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>LRa03</b>, from 30 September 2009 to 1 March 2010 – 5289 stars observed;</li>
<li><b>SRa03</b>, from 1 March 2010 to 2 April 2010;</li>
<li><b>LRc05</b>, from 2 April 2010 to 5 July 2010;</li>
<li><b>LRc06</b>, from 5 July 2010 to 27 September 2010;</li>
<li><b>LRa04</b>, from 27 September 2010 to 16 December 2010;</li>
<li><b>LRa05</b>, from 16 December 2010 to 5 April 2011;</li>
<li><b>LRc07</b>, from 5 April 2011 to 30 June 2011;</li>
<li><b>SRc03</b>, from 1 July 2011 to 5 July 2011 – a run made to reobserve the transit of <a href="/wiki/COROT-9b" title="COROT-9b">COROT-9b</a>;</li>
<li><b>LRc08</b>, from 6 July 2011 to 30 September 2011;</li>
<li><b>SRa04</b>, from 30 September 2011 to 28 November 2011;</li>
<li><b>SRa05</b>, from 29 November 2011 to 9 January 2012;</li>
<li><b>LRa06</b>, from 10 January 2012 to 29 March 2012 – a run dedicated to reobservation of <a href="/wiki/COROT-7b" title="COROT-7b">COROT-7b</a>;</li>
<li><b>LRc09</b>, from 10 April 2012, in progress.</li>
</ul>
<p>The probe monitors the brightness of stars over time, searching for the slight dimming that happens in regular intervals when planets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_transit" title="Astronomical transit">transit</a> their primary sun. In every field, CoRoT records the brightness of thousands stars in the <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">V-magnitude</a> range from 11 to 16 for the extrasolar planet study. In fact, stellar targets brighter than 11 will saturate the exoplanets CCD detectors, yielding inaccurate data, whilst stars dimmer than 16 don't deliver enough <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> to allow planetary detections. CoRoT will be sensitive enough to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocky_planet" title="Rocky planet">rocky planets</a> with a radius two times larger than Earth, orbiting stars brighter than 14;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> it is also expected to discover new gas giants in the whole magnitude range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-detection_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-detection-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>CoRoT also undertakes <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a>. It can detect <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a> variations associated with acoustic pulsations of stars. This phenomenon allows calculation of a star's precise mass, age and chemical composition and will aid in comparisons between the sun and other stars. For this program, in each field of view there will be one main target star for asteroseismology as well as up to nine other targets. The number of observed targets have dropped to half after the loss of Data Processing Unit No. 1.</p>
<p>The mission began on 27 December 2006 when a Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soyuz_2_rocket" title="Soyuz 2 rocket">Soyuz 2-1b</a> rocket lifted the satellite into a circular <a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">polar orbit</a> with an altitude of 827 km . The first scientific observation campaign started on 3 February 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNESPR2007_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNESPR2007-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Until March 2013, the mission's cost will amount to €170 million, of which 75% is paid by the French space agency CNES and 25% is contributed by Austria, Belgium, Germany, Spain, Brazil and the European Space Agency ESA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The primary contractor for the construction of the CoRoT vehicle was CNES,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CameraPR2005_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-CameraPR2005-21">[21]</a></sup> to which individual components were delivered for vehicle assembly. The COROT equipment bay, which houses the data acquisition and pre-processing electronics, was constructed by the LESIA Laboratory at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Observatory" title="Paris Observatory">Paris Observatory</a> and took 60 <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/man-year" title="wikt:man-year">person-years</a> to complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CameraPR2005_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-CameraPR2005-21">[21]</a></sup> The design and building of the instruments were done by the Laboratoire d'études spatiales et d'instrumentation en astrophysique (LESIA) de l'<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observatoire_de_Paris" title="Observatoire de Paris">Observatoire de Paris</a>, the Laboratoire d'Astrophysique de Marseille, the Institut d'Astrophysique Spatiale (IAS) from Orsay, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centre_spatial_de_Li%C3%A8ge" title="Centre spatial de Liège">Centre spatial de Liège</a> (CSL) in Belgium, the IWF in Austria, the DLR (Berlin) in Germany and the ESA Research and Science Support Department. The 30 cm afocal telescope Corotel has been realized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcatel_Alenia_Space" title="Alcatel Alenia Space">Alcatel Alenia Space</a> in the Centre spatial de Cannes Mandelieu.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential">Potential</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Potential">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Before the beginning of the mission, the team stated with caution that CoRoT would only be able to detect planets few times larger than Earth or greater, and that it was not specifically designed to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_planet" title="Habitable planet">habitable planets</a>. According to the press release announcing the first results, CoRoT's instruments are performing with higher precision than had been predicted, and may be able to find planets down to the size of Earth with short orbits around small stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAFirstPlanet_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-ESAFirstPlanet-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit method</a> requires the detection of at least two transits, hence the planets detected will mostly have an orbital period under 75-day. Candidates that show only one transit have been found, but uncertainty remains about their exact orbital period.</p>
<p>CoRoT should be assumed to detect a small percentage of planets within the observed star fields, due to the low percentage of exoplanets that would transit from the angle of observation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. The chances of seeing a planet transiting its host star is inversely proportional to the diameter of the planet's orbit, thus close in planets detections will outnumber outer planets ones. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit method</a> is also biased toward large planets, since their very depth transits are more easily detected than the shallows eclipses induced by terrestrial planets.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_of_Data_Processing_Unit_.231">Failure of Data Processing Unit #1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Failure of Data Processing Unit #1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 8 March 2009 the satellite suffered a loss of communication with Data Processing Unit No. 1, processing data from one of the two photo-detector chains on the spacecraft. Science operations resumed early April with Data Processing Unit No. 1 offline while Data Processing Unit No. 2 operating normally. The loss of photo-detector chain number 1 results in the loss of one <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a> dedicated to planet detection. The <a href="/wiki/Field_of_view" title="Field of view">field of view</a> of the satellite is thus reduced by 50%, but without any degradation of the quality of the observations. The loss of channel 1 appears to be permanent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dp1_loss_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-dp1_loss-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Follow-up_program">Follow-up program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Follow-up program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The rate of discoveries of transiting planets is dictated by the need of ground based follow-up observations, needed to verify the planetary nature of the transit candidates. Candidate detections have been obtained for about 2.3% of all CoRoT targets, but finding <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">periodic</a> transit events isn't enough to claim a planet discovery, since several configurations could mimic a transiting planet, such as <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">stellar binaries</a>, or an eclipsing fainter star very close to the target star, whose light, blended in the light curve, can reproduce transit-like events. A first screening is executed on the light curves, searching hints of secondary eclipses or a rather V-shaped transit, indicative of a stellar nature of the transits. For the brighter targets, the prism in front of the exoplanets CCDs provides photometry in 3 different colors, enabling to reject planet candidates that have different transit depths in the three channels, a behaviour typical of binary stars. These tests allow to discard 83% of the candidate detections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> whilst the remaining 17% are screened with photometric and radial velocity follow-up from a network of telescopes around the world. Photometric observations, required to rule out a possible contamination by a diluted eclipsing binary in close vicinity of the target,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> is performed on several 1 m-class instruments, but also employs the 2 m <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tautenburg_Observatory" title="Tautenburg Observatory">Tautenburg telescope</a> in Germany and the 3,6 m <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93France%E2%80%93Hawaii_Telescope" title="Canada–France–Hawaii Telescope">CFHT/Megacam</a> in Hawaii. The radial velocity follow-up allows to discard binaries or even <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">multiple star system</a> and, given enough observations, provide the <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> of the exoplanets found. Radial velocity follow-up is performed with high-precision <a href="/wiki/Spectrograph" title="Spectrograph">spectrographs</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/SOPHIE_%C3%A9chelle_spectrograph" title="SOPHIE échelle spectrograph">SOPHIE</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HARPS" title="HARPS">HARPS</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIRES" title="HIRES">HIRES</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Once the planetary nature of the candidate is established, high-resolution <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> is performed on the host star, in order to accurately determine the stellar parameters, from which further exoplanet characteristics can be derived. Such work is done with large aperture telescopes, as the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">UVES spectrograph</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HIRES" title="HIRES">HIRES</a>.</p>
<p>Interesting transiting planets could be further followed-up with the infrared <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer</a> Space Telescope, to give an independent confirmation at a different <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">wavelength</a> and possibly detect reflected light from the planet or the atmospheric compositions. <a href="/wiki/COROT-7b" title="COROT-7b">COROT-7b</a> and <a href="/wiki/COROT-9b" title="COROT-9b">COROT-9b</a> have already been observed by <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer</a>.</p>
<p>Papers presenting the results of follow-up operations of planetary candidates in the IRa01,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> LRc01,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> LRa01,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> SRc01<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> fields have been published. Sometimes the faintness of the target star or its characteristics, such as a high rotational velocity or strong stellar activity, do not allow to determine unambiguously the nature or the mass of the planetary candidate.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discoveries">Discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroseismology_and_stellar_physics">Asteroseismology and stellar physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Asteroseismology and stellar physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Stars vibrate according to many different pulsation modes in much the same way that musical instruments emit a variety of sounds. Listening to an air on the guitar does not leave any doubt as to the nature of the instrument, and an experienced musician can even deduce the cords' material and tension. Similarly, stellar pulsation modes are characteristic of global stellar properties and of the internal physical conditions. Analyzing these modes is thus a way of probing stellar interiors to infer stellar chemical composition, rotation profiles and internal physical properties such as temperatures and densities. <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">Asteroseismology</a> is the science which studies the vibration modes of a star. Each of these modes can be mathematically represented by a <a href="/wiki/Spherical_harmonics" title="Spherical harmonics">spherical harmonic</a> of degree l and azimuthal order m. Some examples are presented here below with a color scheme in which blue (red) indicates contracting (expanding) material (<a class="external text" href="http://www.ago.ulg.ac.be/Cnt/fiche_f.php?qLbl=Salm01" rel="nofollow">Sébastien Salmon</a>). The pulsation amplitudes are highly exaggerated.</p>

<div class="thumb tright">
