</tr>
</table>
<p>When applied to the Sun, this science is called <a href="/wiki/Helioseismology" title="Helioseismology">helioseismology</a> and has been ongoing for a few decades by now. The solar surface helium abundance was derived very accurately for the first time, which has definitely shown the importance of microscopic diffusion in the solar structure. Helioseismology analyses have also unveiled the solar internal rotational profile, the precise extent of the convective envelope and the location of the helium ionization zone. Despite enormous technical challenges, it was thus tempting to apply similar analyses to stars. From the ground this was only possible for stars close to the Sun such as α Centauri, Procyon, β Virginis... The goal is to detect extremely small light variations (down to 1 ppm) and to extract the frequencies responsible for these brightness fluctuations. This produces a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_spectrum" title="Frequency spectrum">frequency spectrum</a> typical of the star under scrutiny. Oscillation periods vary from a few minutes to several hours depending of the type of star and its evolutionary state. To reach such performances, long observing times devoid of day/night alternations are required. Space is thus the ideal asteroseismic laboratory. By revealing their microvariability, measuring their oscillations at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million#Parts-per_expressions" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> level, CoRoT has provided a new vision of stars, never reached before by any ground-based observation.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CoRoT_HR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="833" data-file-width="1127" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/CoRoT_HR.jpg/300px-CoRoT_HR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/CoRoT_HR.jpg/450px-CoRoT_HR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/CoRoT_HR.jpg/600px-CoRoT_HR.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CoRoT_HR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dwarf and giant stars observed by CoRoT in the sismo and exo fields with some additional stars observed from the ground. From the work of members of the CoRoT team</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the beginning of the mission, two out of four CCDs were assigned to asteroseismic observations of bright stars (apparent magnitude 6 to 9) in the so-called <i>sismo field</i> while the other CCDs were reserved for exoplanet hunting in the so-called <i>exo field</i>. Albeit with a lower <a href="/wiki/Signal-to-noise_ratio" title="Signal-to-noise ratio">signal to noise ratio</a>, interesting science on stars was also obtained from the exoplanets channel data, where the probe records several thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_curves" title="Light curves">light curves</a> from every observed field. Stellar activity, rotation periods, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_spots" title="Sun spots">star spot</a> evolution, star–planet interactions, <a href="/wiki/Star_system" title="Star system">multiple star systems</a> are nice extras in addition to the main asteroseismic program. This exo field also turned out to be of incalculable richness in asteroseismic discoveries. During the first six years of its mission, CoRoT has observed about 150 bright stars in the <i>sismo field</i> and more than 150 000 weak stars in the <i>exo field</i>. The figure shows where most of them are located in the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a> together with some others observed from the ground.</p>
<p>Discoveries were numerous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Let us cite the first detection of <a href="/wiki/Helioseismology" title="Helioseismology">solar-like oscillations</a> in stars other than the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michel_322_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michel_322-31">[31]</a></sup> the first detection of non-radial oscillations in red giant stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ridder.2C_J._2009_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ridder.2C_J._2009-32">[32]</a></sup> the detection of solar-like oscillations in massive stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belkacem.2C_K._2009_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belkacem.2C_K._2009-33">[33]</a></sup> <b>·</b> ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Degroote.2C_P._2010_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Degroote.2C_P._2010-34">[34]</a></sup> the discovery of hundreds of frequencies in <a href="/wiki/Delta_Scuti_variable" title="Delta Scuti variable">δ Scuti stars</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> the spectacular time evolution of the frequency spectrum of a Be (emission lines B) star during an outburst,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> the first detection of a deviation from a constant period spacing in gravity modes in an SPB (Slowly Pulsating B) star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">[37]</a></sup> Interpreting those results opened new horizons in our vision of stars and galaxies. In October 2009 the CoRoT mission was the subject of a special issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Astronomy_and_Astrophysics" title="Astronomy and Astrophysics">Astronomy and Astrophysics</a></i>, dedicated to the early results of the probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Below are some examples of breakthrough contributions to stellar astrophysics, based on CoRoT's data:</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Extension_of_the_chemically_mixed_zone_in_main_sequence_stars">Extension of the chemically mixed zone in main sequence stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Extension of the chemically mixed zone in main sequence stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Above the convective core where mixing of chemicals is instantaneous and efficient, some layers can be affected by partial or total mixing during the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> phase of evolution. The extent of this <i>extra mixed zone</i> as well as the mixing efficiency are, however, difficult to assess. This additional mixing has very important consequences since it involves longer time scales for nuclear burning phases and may in particular affect the value of the stellar mass at the transition between those stars which end up their life as <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a> and those which face a final supernova explosion. The impact on the chemical evolution of the galaxy is obvious. Physical reasons for this extra-mixing are various, either a mixing induced by internal rotation or a mixing resulting from convective bubbles crossing the convective core boundary to enter the radiative zone where they finally lose their identity (overshooting), or even some other poorly known processes.</p>
<ol>
<li><b>Solar-like stars:</b> The solar-like star HD 49933 is illustrative of this extra-mixing problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Its convective envelope is responsible for the presence of <a href="/wiki/Helioseismology" title="Helioseismology">solar-like oscillations</a>. Comparing the observed frequency spectrum with that obtained from theoretical models of 1.19 M<sub>ʘ</sub> computed with and without additional mixing clearly excludes a model without extra mixing.</li>
<li><b>Sub-giant stars:</b> Such an additional mixing also affects the structure of more evolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-giant" title="Sub-giant">sub-giant</a> stars since the mass extension of the helium core formed during core hydrogen burning is increased. The sub-giant star HD 49385 of 1.3 M<sub>ʘ</sub> was submitted to CoRoT scrutiny and although not fully conclusive, new constraints were brought to the modeling of such stars.</li>
<li><b>SPB stars:</b> More massive SPB (Slowly Pulsating B) stars show a frequency spectrum dominated by high order gravity modes excited by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9A_mechanism" title="Κ mechanism">κ mechanism</a> at work in layers where ionizations of iron group elements produces an opacity peak. In such stars, the convective core is surrounded by a region of varying chemical composition, the so-called μ-gradient region, left by the progressive withdrawal of the convective core as hydrogen is transformed into helium. This region is rather thin and constitutes a sharp transition region, which induces a very subtle signature in the gravity modes frequency spectrum. Instead of a constant period spacing found in a homogeneous stellar model, periodic deviations from this constant value are expected in models affected by a sharp transition region. Moreover, the period of the deviations is directly related to the precise location of the sharp transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This phenomenon has been detected in two hybrid B stars (showing at the same time acoustic β Cephei and gravity SPB modes): (1) HD 50230<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-37">[37]</a></sup> where an extra-mixing with a somewhat smooth shape is clearly required in the modeling and (2) HD 43317.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_of_the_superficial_stellar_layers">Structure of the superficial stellar layers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Structure of the superficial stellar layers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ol>
<li><b>Transition layers in stellar envelopes:</b> Transition layers such as the helium ionization region or the lower boundary of the convective envelope in low mass and red giant stars also affect frequency spectra. In a structure devoid of such discontinuities, high order acoustic modes obey some regularities in their frequency distribution (large frequency separation, second difference...). Transition zones introduce periodic deviations with respect to these regularities and the periods of the deviations are directly related to the precise location of the transition zones. These deviations were predicted by theory and were first observed in the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Thanks to CoRoT they were also detected in the solar-like star HD 49933<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and also in the red giant star HD 181907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In both cases the location of the helium ionization zone could be accurately derived.</li>
<li><b>Amplitudes and line widths in solar-like oscillation spectra:</b> One of the major successes of the CoRoT space mission has definitely been the detection of solar-like oscillations in stars slightly hotter than the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michel_322_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michel_322-31">[31]</a></sup> As was previously done for the Sun, measurements of amplitudes and line widths in their frequency spectra resulted in new constraints in the modeling of <a href="/wiki/Solar-like_oscillations" title="Solar-like oscillations">stochastic excitations</a> of acoustic modes by turbulent convection. The frequency spectrum of HD 49933<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> was confronted to the stochastic excitation model developed by Samadi et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Except at high frequencies, a good agreement can be reached by adopting a <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> ten times smaller than the solar metallicity. With the solar value on the contrary, disagreements in amplitudes can reach a factor 2 at low frequencies.</li>
<li><b>Granulation:</b> The presence of granulation was detected in the frequency spectrum of HD 49933. Analyses have been done with 3D hydrodynamical model atmospheres computed at solar and ten times smaller than solar metallicities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Here again the model with the lowest metallicity shows up to be closer to the observations although significant disagreements still remain.</li>
</ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Red_giants_and_chemical_evolution_of_our_galaxy">Red giants and chemical evolution of our galaxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Red giants and chemical evolution of our galaxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following exhaustion of hydrogen in the core, the overall stellar structure drastically changes. Hydrogen burning now takes place in a narrow shell surrounding the newly processed helium core. While the helium core quickly contracts and heats up, the layers above the hydrogen-burning shell undergo important expansion and cooling. The star becomes a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> whose radius and luminosity increase in time. These stars are now located on the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_giant_branch" title="Red giant branch">red giant branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a>; they are commonly named <i>RGB stars</i>. Once their central temperature reaches 100 10<sup>6</sup> K, helium starts burning in the core. For stellar masses smaller than about 2 M<sub>ʘ</sub>, this new combustion takes place in a highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneracy_pressure" title="Degeneracy pressure">degenerate</a> matter and proceeds through a <a href="/wiki/Helium_flash" title="Helium flash">helium flash</a>. The readjustment following the flash brings the red giant to the so-called <a href="/wiki/Red_clump" title="Red clump">red clump</a> (RC) in the Hertzsprung-Russell diagram.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Histograms.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="519" data-file-width="660" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Histograms.png/300px-Histograms.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Histograms.png/450px-Histograms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Histograms.png/600px-Histograms.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Histograms.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Histograms of a synthetic red giant population (in red) and CoRoT red giant population (in orange). From <a class="external text" href="http://www.birmingham.ac.uk/schools/physics/people/staff-profile.aspx?ReferenceId=15800&amp;Name=dr-andrea-miglio" rel="nofollow">Andrea Miglio</a> and collaborators</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="924" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png/300px-Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png/450px-Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png/600px-Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carte_3D_de_notre_Galaxie.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
3D map of our galaxy from seismic data of red giants observed by CoRoT. From <a class="external text" href="http://www.birmingham.ac.uk/schools/physics/people/staff-profile.aspx?ReferenceId=15800&amp;Name=dr-andrea-miglio" rel="nofollow">Andrea Miglio</a> and collaborators</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Whether RGB or RC, these stars all have an extended convective envelope favorable to the excitation of solar-like oscillations. A major success of CoRoT has been the discovery of radial and long-lived non-radial oscillations in thousands of red giants in the exo field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ridder.2C_J._2009_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ridder.2C_J._2009-32">[32]</a></sup> For each of them, the frequency at maximum power ν<sub>max</sub> in the frequency spectrum as well as the large frequency separation between consecutive modes Δν could be measured,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> defining a sort of individual seismic passport.</p>
<ol>
<li><b>Red giant population in our galaxy:</b> Introducing these seismic signatures, together with an estimation of the effective temperature, in the scaling laws relating them to the global stellar properties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravities</a> (seismic gravities), masses and radii can be estimated and luminosities and distances immediately follow for those thousands of red giants. <a href="/wiki/Histogram" title="Histogram">Histograms</a> could then be drawn and a totally unexpected and spectacular result came out when comparing these CoRoT histograms with theoretical ones obtained from theoretical synthetic populations of red giants in our galaxy. Such theoretical populations were computed from stellar evolution models, with adopting various hypotheses to describe the successive generations of stars along the time evolution of our galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a class="external text" href="http://www.birmingham.ac.uk/schools/physics/people/staff-profile.aspx?ReferenceId=15800&amp;Name=dr-andrea-miglio" rel="nofollow">Andrea Miglio</a> and collaborators noticed that both types of histograms were spitting images of one another,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> as can be seen in the histograms picture. Moreover, adding the knowledge of the distances of these thousands of stars to their galactic coordinates, a 3D map of our galaxy was drawn. This is illustrated in the figure where different colors relate to different CoRoT runs and to <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)"><i>Kepler</i></a> observations (green points).</li>
<li><b>Age-metallicity relation in our galaxy:</b> The age of a red giant is closely related to its former main sequence lifetime, which is in turn determined by its mass and metallicity. Knowing the mass of a red giant amounts to knowing its age. If the metallicity is known the uncertainty in age does not exceed 15%! Observational missions such as <a class="external text" href="http://www.sdss3.org/surveys/apogee.php" rel="nofollow">APOGEE</a> <i>(Apache Point Observatoty Galactic Evolution Environment)</i> whose goal is to measure metallicities for 100 000 red giants in our galaxy, <a class="external text" href="https://galah-survey.org/" rel="nofollow">GALAH</a> <i>(Galactic Archaeology with HERMES)</i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GAIA_(satellite)" title="GAIA (satellite)">GAIA</a> <i>(Global Astrometric Interferometer for Astrophysics)</i> could of course widely benefit from these seismic gravities with the ultimate output of establishing the age-metallicity relation in our galaxy. Asteroseismology has crossed the doorstep of the structure and chemical evolution of our galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Seismic signatures and extension of mixed zones during central hydrogen and helium burning:</b> Increasing even further the scrutiny in analyzing the CoRoT<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and <i>Kepler</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> frequency spectra of red giants brought new important discoveries. Small and subtle differences in seismic signatures allow us to distinguish RGB from RC stars notwithstanding their similar luminosities. This is now theoretically confirmed thanks to elaborate red giant modeling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The period spacings of gravity-dominated modes are expected to be especially meaningful. Their detection for a large number of red giants could give us clues to establishing the extent of the extra-mixed region above the convective core during core hydrogen burning, but also the extent of the extra-mixed region during core helium burning, both mixing processes being <i>a priori</i> totally unrelated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Massive_stars">Massive stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Massive stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Massive variable main sequence stars have frequency spectra dominated by acoustic modes excited by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9A_mechanism" title="Κ mechanism">κ mechanism</a> at work in layers where partial ionization of iron group elements produce a peak in opacity. In addition the most advanced of these stars present mixed modes i.e. modes with a g-character in deep layers and p-character in the envelope. Hydrogen burning takes place in a convective core surrounded by a region of varying chemical composition and an envelope mostly radiative except for tiny convective layers related to partial ionization of helium and/or iron group elements. As in lower mass stars the extent of the fully or partially mixed region located just above the convective core <i>(extra-mixed zone)</i> is one of the main uncertainties affecting theoretical modeling.</p>
<ol>
<li><b>β Cephei stars:</b> Seismic analyses of <a href="/wiki/Beta_Cephei_variable" title="Beta Cephei variable">β Cephei</a> stars show that it is not obvious to derive a one-to-one extent of this extra-mixed zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A rather large extent seems to be required to model θ Ophiuchi<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> while a much smaller one is favored for HD 129929,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> for β Canis Majoris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> for δ Ceti,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and for 12 Lacertae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> This extra-mixed zone could even be absent in the structure of V1449 Aquilae (HD 180642)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and ν Eridani.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It would be extremely interesting to establish a relation between the extent of this zone and the rotation velocity and/or the magnetic field of the star. Seismic analysis of V2052 Ophiuchi<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> shows that this star although rapidly rotating, which would favor extra-mixing, could be devoid of such a region. The magnetic field detected in this star could be the reason of this lack of extra-mixing.</li>
<li><b>Be stars:</b> Late Be type stars HD 181231 and HD 175869 are very rapid rotators, about 20 times more rapid than the Sun. Their seismic analysis seems to require a centrally mixed zone about 20% larger than what is expected from convection only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Another Be star, HD 49330, had a very exciting surprise in store. Observed by CoRoT during an outburst of matter towards its circumstellar disk, which is typical of such stars, its frequency spectrum suffered drastic changes. Firstly dominated by acoustic modes the spectrum showed the appearance of gravity modes with amplitudes strictly in line with the outburst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Such a link between the nature of the excited modes and a dynamical phenomenon is of course a gold mine in our quest for the internal structure of Be stars.</li>
<li><b>O stars:</b> A bunch of O stars have been observed by CoRoT. Among them HD 46150 and HD 46223 (members of the galactic cluster NGC 2264) and HD 46966 (member of the OB association Mon OB2) do not seem to pulsate, which is in agreement with stellar modeling of stars with similar global parameters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The frequency spectrum of the <a href="/wiki/Plaskett%27s_star" title="Plaskett's star">Plaskett's star</a> HD 47129 on the contrary shows a peak with six harmonics in the frequency range expected from theoretical modeling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>Another unexpected CoRoT discovery was the presence of solar-like oscillations in massive stars. The small convective shell related to the opacity peak resulting from the ionization of iron group elements at about 200 000 K (iron opacity peak) could indeed be responsible for the stochastic excitation of acoustic modes like those observed in our Sun.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="727" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png/300px-Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png/450px-Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png/600px-Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chimera-V1449_Aqu.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Frequency versus time for a solar-like mode (top) and a beta Cephei mode (bottom) in Chimera. From Kevin Belkacem, Frédéric Baudin and collaborators</div>
</div>
</div>
<ol>
<li><b>V1449 Aquilae (HD 180642):</b> This CoRoT target is a β Cephei star whose frequency spectrum reveals high frequency and very small amplitude acoustic modes. A careful analysis has shown that they were solar-like oscillations excited by turbulent bubbles origination from this convective iron opacity peak zone or even from the convective core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belkacem.2C_K._2009_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belkacem.2C_K._2009-33">[33]</a></sup> This is indeed a major discovery since it was the first time that pulsations excited by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%9A_mechanism" title="Κ mechanism">κ mechanism</a> acting in the iron opacity peak zone were present side by side in the same star with pulsations stochastically excited by this very same zone. This is the reason why Kevin Belkacem, main discoverer of these solar-like oscillations in V1449 Aquilae, added a new baptismal certificate to this β Cephei star and named it <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(mythology)" title="Chimera (mythology)"><i>Chimera</i></a>. The figure illustrates the behavior of the frequency versus time for two modes in the frequency spectrum of Chimera, a solar-like mode (top) and a β Cephei mode (bottom). The stochastic nature of the solar-like mode reveals itself in the instability of its frequency as time goes on and in the spread in frequency on several μHz. The contrast with the stability in frequency and the narrow frequency range of the β Cephei mode is striking.</li>
<li><b>HD 46149:</b> Later on solar-like oscillations were even discovered in a more massive O star member of the binary system HD 46149.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Degroote.2C_P._2010_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Degroote.2C_P._2010-34">[34]</a></sup> Constraints coming from the binary nature of the system coupled with seismic constraints led to the determination of the orbital parameters of the system as well as to the global properties of its members.</li>
</ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_open_cluster_NGC_2264">The open cluster NGC 2264</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The open cluster NGC 2264">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During a 23-day observing run in March 2008, CoRoT observed 636 members of the young open cluster <a href="/wiki/NGC_2264" title="NGC 2264">NGC 2264</a>. The so-called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christmas_tree_cluster" title="Christmas tree cluster">Christmas tree cluster</a></i>, is located in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Monoceros" title="Monoceros">Monoceros</a> relatively close to us at a distance of about 1800 light years. Its age is estimated to be between 3 and 8 million years. At such a young age, the cluster is an ideal target to investigate many different scientific questions connected to the formation of stars and early stellar evolution. The CoRoT data of stars in NGC 2264 allow us to study the interaction of recently formed stars with their surrounding matter, the rotation and activity of cluster members as well as their distribution, the interiors of young stars by using asteroseismology, and planetary and stellar eclipses.</p>
<p>The stellar births and the stars' childhoods remain mostly hidden from us in the optical light because the early stars are deeply embedded in the dense molecular cloud from which they are born. Observations in the infrared or X-ray enable us to look deeper into the cloud, and learn more about these earliest phases in stellar evolution. Therefore, in December 2011 and January 2012, CoRoT was part of a large international observing campaign involving four space telescopes and several ground based observatories. All instruments observed about 4000 stars in the young cluster NGC 2264 simultaneously for about one month at different wavelengths. The Canadian space mission <a href="/wiki/MOST_(satellite)" title="MOST (satellite)">MOST</a> targeted the brightest stars in the cluster in the optical light, while CoRoT observed the fainter members. MOST and CoRoT observed NGC 2264 continuously for 39 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The NASA satellites <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra</a> measured at the same time the stars in the infrared (for 30 days) and the X-ray domains (for 300 kiloseconds). Ground-based observations were taken also at the same time, for example, with the ESO <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">Very Large Telescope</a> in Chile, the <a class="external text" href="http://www.cfht.hawaii.edu" rel="nofollow">Canadian-French-Hawaiian Telescope</a> in Hawaii, the <a href="/wiki/McDonald_Observatory" title="McDonald Observatory">McDonald Observatory</a> in Texas, or the <a href="/wiki/Calar_Alto_Observatory" title="Calar Alto Observatory">Calar Alto Observatory</a> in Spain.</p>
<p>The CoRoT observations led to the discovery of about a dozen pulsating <a href="/wiki/Pre-main-sequence_star" title="Pre-main-sequence star">pre-main sequence</a> (PMS) δ Scuti stars and the confirmation of the existence of γ Doradus pulsations in PMS stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Also the presence of hybrid δ Scuti/γ Doradus pulsations was confirmed in members of NGC 2264. The CoRoT observations included also the well known pre-main sequence pulsators, V 588 Mon and V 589 Mon, which were the first discovered members of this group of stars. The precision attained in the CoRoT light curves also revealed the important role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granulation_(solar_physics)" title="Granulation (solar physics)">granulation</a> in pre-main sequence stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The investigation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_Tauri_type_stars" title="T Tauri type stars">T Tauri</a> stars and their interaction with their circumstellar matter using CoRoT data revealed the existence of a new class, the <a href="/wiki/AA_Tauri" title="AA Tauri">AA Tauri</a> type objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Previously to the CoRoT observations, T Tauri stars were known to either show sinusoidal light variations that are caused by spots on the stellar surface, or completely irregular variability that is caused by the gas and dust disks surrounding the young stars. AA Tauri type objects show periodically occurring minima that are different in depth and width, hence are semi-regular variables. With the CoRoT observations this class of objects could be established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Exciting insights into the earliest phases of stellar evolution also come from the comparison of the variability present in the optical light to that in the infrared and the X-ray regime.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_systems">Binary systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Binary systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A large number of <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary systems</a> with non-radially pulsating members were observed by CoRoT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Some of them, which were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclipsing_binary" title="Eclipsing binary">eclipsing binaries</a> with members of <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Doradus_variable" title="Gamma Doradus variable">γ Doradus</a> type, were discovered during CoRoT runs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The eclipse phenomenon plays a key role since global parameters can immediately follow, bringing invaluable constraints, in addition to the seismic ones, to stellar modeling.</p>
<ol>
<li><b>AU Monocerotis:</b> This semi-detached binary system contains a <a href="/wiki/Be_star" title="Be star">Be star</a> interacting with its G star companion. Its observation by CoRoT provided an extremely high quality ligthcurve. Global parameters could then be improved and new <a href="/wiki/Ephemeris" title="Ephemeris">ephemeris</a> for the orbital motion as well as for another long term variation were derived. This long period variation seems to originate from a periodic light attenuation by circumstellar dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HD_174884.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1421" data-file-width="1311" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/HD_174884.png/300px-HD_174884.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/HD_174884.png/450px-HD_174884.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/HD_174884.png/600px-HD_174884.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HD_174884.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The light curve of HD 174884. The upper panel shows the full light curve. The second panel is a blow-up where tiny secondary minima are visible (their depth is 1% of the deeper minimum). The third panel shows the projection on the plane of the sky (i.e. as we see the system) at different phases. From Carla Maceroni and the CoRoT binary team</div>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li><b>HD 174884:</b> Tidally induced pulsations have been detected in the high eccentricity (e=0.29) and short period binary system HD 174884 consisting of two B stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The upper panel of the figure shows the full light curve of the system. In the second panel tiny secondary eclipses are seen with a depth of about 1% of the depth of the primary eclipse. Actually the system is formed of stars of similar mass, size and temperature. Were the orbit circular, the eclipses would be similar in depth. However the orbit is highly eccentric and its orientation in space with respect to us is such that the secondary eclipse occurs when the stars are at a larger distance than at primary eclipse. The third panel of the figure shows the projection on the plane of the sky (i.e. the system as we see it) at different orbital phases.</li>
<li><b>CoRoT 102918586:</b> The relatively bright eclipsing system CoRoT 102918586 is a double-lined spectroscopic binary, observed by CoRoT, which revealed clear evidence of γ Doradus type pulsations. In addition to CoRoT photometry, a spectroscopic follow-up was performed which yielded the radial velocity curves, the component effective temperatures, the metallicity, and the line-of-sight projected rotational velocities. The eclipsing binary light curve analysis, combined with the spectroscopic results, provided system physical parameters with 1–2% accuracy while the comparison with evolutionary models led to constraints on the age of the system. After subtracting the best–fitting eclipsing binary model, the residuals were analyzed to determine the pulsation properties. The primary star pulsates with typical γ Dor frequencies and shows a period spacing consistent with high order g-modes of degree l=1.</li>
<li><b>HR 6902:</b> The binary system ζ Aurigae HR 6902 containing a red giant and a B star was observed by CoRoT during two runs, which allowed us to fully cover the primary as well as the secondary eclipses. This system is presently being analyzed with the ultimate goal of bringing new constraints on the internal structure of the red giant in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>A low mass binary:</b> One of the binary system observed by CoRoT is of particular interest since the less massive component is a late <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification#Class_M" title="Stellar classification">M</a> star of 0.23 M<sub>⊙</sub> with an estimated effective temperature of about 3000 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The primary component is a 1.5 M<sub>⊙</sub> star MS star.</li>
<li><b>A beaming effect in a binary:</b> A binary system observed by CoRoT showed out of eclipses variations which were interpreted as a <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_beaming" title="Relativistic beaming">beaming effect</a> (also called Doppler boosting). This effect results from the variation in brightness of source approaching or moving away from the observer, with an amplitude proportional to the radial velocity divided by the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The periodic variation in the velocity of an orbiting star will thus produce a periodic beaming variation in the light curve. Such an effect can confirm the binary nature of a system even without any detectable eclipses nor transits. One of the main advantages of the beaming effect is the possibility to determine the radial velocity directly from the light curve but very different luminosities of the binary components are required and a single radial velocity curve can only be obtained as in an SB1 binary system. The out of eclipse variations were modeled with the BEER (Beaming Ellipsoidal Reflection) algorithm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exoplanets">Exoplanets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Exoplanets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2171" data-file-width="3360" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg/300px-Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg/450px-Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg/600px-Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_Planet-hunters_Snapped_at_La_Silla.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Two Planet-hunters Snapped at <a href="/wiki/La_Silla_Observatory" title="La Silla Observatory">La Silla Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>To find extra solar planets, CoRoT uses the method of transits detection. The primary transit is the occultation of a fraction of the light from a star when a celestial object, such as a planet, passes between the star and the observer. Its detection is made possible by the sensitivity of CCD to very small changes in light flux. Corot is capable of detecting changes in brightness of about 1/10,000. Scientists can thus hope finding planets with a size of approximately 2 times that of the Earth with this method, a class of planet called Super-Earth; detection of Corot-7b, whose radius is 1.7 times that of the Earth has shown that these predictions were correct. Corot takes an exposure of 32 seconds duration, each 32 seconds, but the image is not fully transmitted to Earth because the data flow would be too large. The onboard computer performs an important work of data reduction: the field around each target star, previously selected by the exoplanets team, is defined on a certain number of pixels described by a particular mask, the sum all pixels within the mask is then performed and several exposures are added (usually 16, which amounts to an integration time of about 8 minutes) before sending this information to the ground. For some stars, considered particularly of interest, data of each exposure is transmitted every 32 seconds. Such a sampling of 32s or 512s is well suited to the detection of a planetary transit that lasts from a little less than an hour to several hours. A feature of this method is that it requires to detect at least three successive transits separated by two equal time intervals before one can consider a target as a serious candidate. A planet of orbital period <i>T</i> should at least be observed for a time interval between 2<i>T</i> and 3<i>T</i> to have a chance to detect three transits. The distance of the planet to the star ( which is characterized by <i>a</i> the semi-major axis of the elliptical orbit ) is linked to its orbital period by the second law of Kepler / Newton <i>a</i><sup>3</sup> = <i>T</i><sup>2</sup> M<sub>star</sub>, using respectively as units for <i>a</i>, M and <i>T</i>: the distance from the Earth to the Sun (150 million km), the mass of the Sun, the orbital period of the Earth (1 year); this implies that if the observing time is less a year, for example, the orbits of the detectable planets will be significantly smaller than that of the Earth. So, for Corot, due to the maximum duration of 6 months of observation for each star field, only planets closer to their stars than 0.3 Astronomical Units (less than the distance between the Sun and Mercury) can be detected, therefore generally not in the so-called habitable zone. The Kepler mission (NASA) has continuously observed the same field for many years and thus had the ability to detect Earth sized planets located farther from their stars.</p>
<p>The moderate number of exoplanets discovered by Corot (32 during the 6 years of operation), is explained by the fact that a confirmation should absolutely be provided by ground-based telescopes, before any announcement is made. Indeed, in the vast majority of cases, the detection of several transits does not mean the detection of a planet, but rather that of a binary star system, either one that corresponds to a grazing occultation of a star by the other, or that the system is close enough to a bright star (the CoRoT target) and the effect of transit is diluted by the light of this star; in both cases the decrease in brightness is low enough to be compatible with that of a planet passing in front of the stellar disk. To eliminate these cases, one performs observations from the ground using two methods: radial velocity spectroscopy and imaging photometry with a CCD camera. In the first case, the mass of the binary stars is immediately detected and in the second case one can expect to identify in the field the binary system near the target star responsible for the alert: the relative decline of brightness will be greater than the one seen by Corot which adds all the light in the mask defining the field of measurement. In consequence, the COROT exoplanet science team has decided to publish confirmed and fully characterized planets only and not simple candidate lists. This strategy, different from the one pursued by the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler</a> mission, where the candidates are regularly updated and made available to the public, is quite lengthy. On the other hand, the approach also increases the scientific return of the mission, as the set of published COROT discoveries constitute some of the best exoplanetary studies carried out so far.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_planetary_discoveries">Timeline of planetary discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Timeline of planetary discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>COROT discovered its first two planets in 2007: the <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiters</a> <a href="/wiki/COROT-1b" title="COROT-1b">COROT-1b</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/COROT-2b" title="COROT-2b">COROT-2b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESAFirstPlanet_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-ESAFirstPlanet-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Results on <a href="/wiki/Asteroseismology" title="Asteroseismology">asteroseismology</a> were published in the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2008, two new exoplanets of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> size, <a href="/wiki/COROT-4b" title="COROT-4b">COROT-4b</a> and <a href="/wiki/COROT-5b" title="COROT-5b">COROT-5b</a>, as well as an unknown massive celestial object, <a href="/wiki/COROT-3b" title="COROT-3b">COROT-3b</a>, were announced by ESA.</p>
<p>In February 2009, during the First Corot Symposium, the super-earth <a href="/wiki/COROT-7b" title="COROT-7b">COROT-7b</a> was announced, which at the time was the smallest exoplanet to have its diameter confirmed, at 1.58 Earth diameters. The discoveries of a second non-transiting planet in the same system, <a href="/wiki/COROT-7c" title="COROT-7c">COROT-7c</a>, and of a new Hot Jupiter, <a href="/wiki/COROT-6b" title="COROT-6b">COROT-6b</a>, were also announced at the Symposium.</p>
<p>In March 2010 <a href="/wiki/COROT-9b" title="COROT-9b">COROT-9b</a> was announced. It's a long period planet (95.3 days) in an orbit close to that of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2010 the COROT team announced<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> six new planets, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-8b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-8b (page does not exist)">COROT-8b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-10b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-10b (page does not exist)">COROT-10b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-11b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-11b (page does not exist)">COROT-11b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-12b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-12b (page does not exist)">COROT-12b</a>, <a href="/wiki/COROT-13b" title="COROT-13b">COROT-13b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-14b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-14b (page does not exist)">COROT-14b</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-15b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-15b (page does not exist)">COROT-15b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> All the planets announced are Jupiter sized, except <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-8b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-8b (page does not exist)">COROT-8b</a>, which appears to be somewhat between <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>. The probe was also able to tentatively detect the reflected light at optical wavelengths of <a href="/wiki/HD_46375_b" title="HD 46375 b">HD46375 b</a>, a non-transiting planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2011, during the Second Corot Symposium, the probe added ten new objects to the Exoplanet catalogue:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-16b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-16b (page does not exist)">COROT-16b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-17b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-17b (page does not exist)">COROT-17b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-18b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-18b (page does not exist)">COROT-18b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-19b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-19b (page does not exist)">COROT-19b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-20b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-20b (page does not exist)">COROT-20b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-21b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-21b (page does not exist)">COROT-21b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-22b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-22b (page does not exist)">COROT-22b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-23b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-23b (page does not exist)">COROT-23b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-24b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-24b (page does not exist)">COROT-24b</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-24c&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-24c (page does not exist)">COROT-24c</a>.</p>
<p>As of November 2011, around 600 additional candidate exoplanets are being screened for confirmation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_results">Main results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Main results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Among the exoplanets Corot detected, one can highlight a subset with the most original features :</p>
<ul>
<li>Corot-1b, the first planet detected by CoRoT is a hot Jupiter. By further analysis, <a href="/wiki/COROT-1b" title="COROT-1b">COROT-1b</a> became the first exoplanets to have its secondary eclipse detected in the optical,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> thanks to the high precision lightcurve delivered by CoRoT.</li>
<li>Corot-3b, with a mass of 22 M<sub>Jup</sub>, it appears to be "something between a <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a> and a planet." According to the definition of planet proposed by the owners of the exoplanet.eu database<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> three years later, <a href="/wiki/COROT-3b" title="COROT-3b">COROT-3b</a>, being less massive than 25 Jupiter masses, is classified as an exoplanet. In an August 2010 paper, COROT detected the ellipsoidal and the relativistic beaming effects in the <a href="/wiki/COROT-3" title="COROT-3">COROT-3</a> lightcurve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>Corot-7b, with a radius of 1.7 R<sub>Earth</sub> and a mass of 7.3 M<sub>Earth</sub>, was the first confirmed rocky planet, with a density and composition which are close to those of the Earth.
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corot7b_artist_view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3508" data-file-width="4961" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Corot7b_artist_view.jpg/220px-Corot7b_artist_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Corot7b_artist_view.jpg/330px-Corot7b_artist_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Corot7b_artist_view.jpg/440px-Corot7b_artist_view.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corot7b_artist_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's impression of Corot-7b, the first rocky Super-Earth ever discovered thanks to a good estimate of its size and mass and therefore its density. The image shows the ocean of lava that must exist on the hemisphere that faces the star. Author: Fabien Catalano</div>
</div>
</div>
Its orbital period (i.e. its local year) is very short since it lasts just 20.5 h; because the planet is very close to its star (an almost solar type star), its orbit is a mere 6 stellar radius. As the planet must be in synchronous rotation with its orbital motion because of the huge tidal forces it undergoes, it always presents the same hemisphere to the star: as a consequence, the two hemispheres, the enlightened and the dark, exhibit an extreme contrast in temperature (2200K vs 50K) and a huge ocean of lava must occupy a large part of the hot side. A continent of water and nitrogen dioxide ices is probably occupying the dark side. Corot-7b was also the first case of a system discovered by CoRoT, with two super-Earths, one in transit the other not; radial velocity measurements have indeed led to the discovery of CoRoT-7c, a planet of 8.4 M<sub>Earth</sub> and a period of 3.79 days. A third planet is even suspected.</li>
<li>Corot-8b, a planet of the same class as Neptune, with a mass of 0.22 M<sub>Jup</sub>;</li>
<li>Corot-9b, the first planet that has earned the epithet of a temperate planet. With 80% of Jupiter mass, and an orbit similar to the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> one, this is the first transiting temperate planet found known to be similar to those within the Solar System. At the time of the discovery, it was the second longest period exoplanet found in transit, after <a href="/wiki/HD_80606_b" title="HD 80606 b">HD80606 b</a>.</li>
<li>Corot-11b and CoRoT-2b, two inflated planets, with radius 1.4 and 1.5 R<sub>Jup</sub> respectively: the theory does not yet provide a consistent model for such objects;</li>
<li>Corot-15b, a bona fide brown dwarf in orbit;</li>
<li>Corot-10b, CoRoT-16b, CoRoT-20b, CoRoT-23b, four hot Jupiters which are on an eccentric orbits, despite circularization is theoretically predicted for such small orbits: this is a clear constraint on Q<sub>p</sub>, the parameter that quantifies the energy dissipation by tidal forces;</li>
<li>Corot-22b, is notable for its small size, having less than half the mass of Saturn.</li>
<li>Corot-24b and c the second planetary system discovered by CoRoT, with two small planets of 0.10 and 0.17 M<sub>Jup</sub>. The two planets are of Neptune size, and orbit the same star and represented the first multiple <i>transiting</i> system detected by CoRoT.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_planets_discovered">List of planets discovered</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: List of planets discovered">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=COROT-17&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="COROT-17 (page does not exist)">COROT-17</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Scutum" title="Scutum">Scutum</a></td>
<td>18<sup>h</sup> 34<sup>m</sup> 47<sup>s</sup></td>
<td>−06° 36′ 44 ″</td>
<td>15.46</td>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_properties_of_the_exoplanets_discovered_by_CoRoT">Global properties of the exoplanets discovered by CoRoT</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COROT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Global properties of the exoplanets discovered by CoRoT">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1527" data-file-width="2122" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png/300px-Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png/450px-Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png/600px-Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corot_Exoplanets_on_Radius_vs_Mass_diagram.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution of CoRoT planets (red circles) in the Radius / Mass diagram. Yellow symbols are the other planets discovered by transit methods</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MstarVsMpl_en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="1966" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/MstarVsMpl_en.png/300px-MstarVsMpl_en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/MstarVsMpl_en.png/450px-MstarVsMpl_en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/MstarVsMpl_en.png/600px-MstarVsMpl_en.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MstarVsMpl_en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of the mass of the star as a function of the planetary mass for CoRoT planets (red) and the other planets discovered by the transit method (yellow). The line across CoRoT data indicates a trend: massive planets are found around massive stars.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All Corot planets were detected during long runs <i>i.e.</i> of at least 70 days. The <i>detection team</i> found on average between 200 and 300 cases of periodic events for each run, corresponding to 2–3% of the stars monitored. Of these, only 530 in total were selected as candidate planets (223 in the direction of the galactic anti-center and 307 towards the center ). Only 30 of them were finally found to be true planets, <i>i.e.</i> about 6%, other cases being eclipsing binaries ( 46%) or unresolved cases (48%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corot_candidates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="851" data-file-width="1424" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Corot_candidates.jpg/330px-Corot_candidates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Corot_candidates.jpg/495px-Corot_candidates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Corot_candidates.jpg/660px-Corot_candidates.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corot_candidates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fig D. The timing and transit depth of all CoRoT planet candidates (courtesy A. Santerne). The size of the symbols indicates the apparent brightness of its parent star (small meaning faint).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The detection capabilities of Corot are illustrated by the figure D showing the depth of the transits measured for all candidates, depending on the period and the brightness of the star: there is indeed a better ability to detect small planets (up to 1.5 R <sub>Earth</sub> ) for short periods (less than 5 days) and bright stars.</p>
<p>The CoRoT planets cover the wide range of properties and features found in the disparate family of exoplanets: for instance, the masses of CoRoT planets cover a range of almost four orders of magnitude, as shown on Figure.</p>
<p>Tracing the mass of the planet versus the mass of the star (Figure), one finds that the CoRoT data set, with its lower scatter than other experiments, indicates a clear trend that massive planets tend to orbit massive stars, which is consistent with the most commonly accepted models of planetary formation.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
